0553801473|3.0|This book fell well short of expectations. A Game of Thrones is my favorite book (not the series, just the book) because it is written so realistically with great characters with vibrant personalities whose actions made sense within the context of those personalities. Eddard dies because of his honor, Robert dies not caring about governing but hunting for a boar, Sansa lies for Joffery because she is a naive little girl, etc. The characters are real, with real strengths and flaws. Almost all the major players/characters are introduced in the beginning, what with the feast at Winterfell for Robert and then on the way to King's Landing. We got to see all the characters together, so even when they diverge we are bound to their stories by where they came from, by common ground. Enough major events happen to keep us hooked: Bran being thrown by Jamie, Catelyn capturing Tyrion at swordpoint, Eddard being attacked by Jamie, etc. I could go on and on.<br /><br />All the stories were tied together by a common purpose: Westeros. Eddard and the other POV's were about Lannisters trying to control the realm, John Snow's was about defending the realm from the Others, and Daenerys was about regaining the realm. Everything is about the realm. The realm is the most important thing that ties everything together. We care about the realm.<br /><br />A Game of Thrones was tightly written. I do not consider a fantasy book, but a book on political intrigue with a gorgeous backstory (Robert's rebellion) in a fantasy setting. There wasn't overt use of magical powers or mystical creatures. Everything was written in a plausible, easy-to-relate to way in the context of political intrigue that could have happened in medieval Europe. The Others and the Dragons and the witchcraft of the woman who killed Dany's child Rhaego were done tastefully. The overall story and plot and characters carried the book, not the fantasy/magical parts of it. Now, don't get me wrong, I don't dislike fantasy obviously, but everything has changed with Dance.<br /><br />With the way the series has progressed, the fantasy is used way too much. There are so many things to keep track of, so many powers and magical artifacts. The Horn of Winter, Euron's Dragon Hellhorn, the Greenseers, the Weirwoods, Warging, Green Dreams, Dragonglass killing the Others, weird wizards in Qarth and the Undying, Lightbringer and AA, burning and seeing things in the fire from R'Hollar, etc. This is all well and fine, but it completely changes the rules. Basically in Game of Thrones, we are limited to what men can accomplish. Those were the rules of the game of thrones. Now, all those rules are thrown out of whack because we have all these new mechanics. A lot of these things have just been mentioned, not necessarily used, but I feel they take away from the character interaction aspects of the story in Game of Thrones. It would have been better to keep the series somewhat within the normal limits of what medieval men could do but then add a bit of fantasy to lean on. The only thing that should have been a trump card the whole time (due to Targaryen precedent) is the dragons, and they haven't even been used yet.<br /><br />The veering towards more overt fantasy and getting away from the core political intrigue might just be my personal dislike, which is fine. What is not fine, however, and something that many people have already brought up, is the entire lack of coherent plot. I mentioned before that A Game of Thrones was all about the realm. It gave us a single cohesive thing that we care about. Everything flowed and made sense and came back to this central aspect. There was a sequence of events that we clearly saw. For example, Catelyn goes down to King's Landing because she gets the dagger. Littlefinger convinces her it was Tyrion's. Catelyn captures Tyrion. In return, Jamie attacks and wounds Eddard. This is shone through several different character's POV's and flows. Obviously this is one story arc,but so much happens. In Dance, we have the Meereenese story arc. Nothing flows at all. The characters involved are Connington and co, Tyrion, Dany, Barristan, and Quentyn. Connington and co just separate, abandoning Meereen completely. Tyrion goes on his massive adventure of ultimately doing nothing and making it to Meereen as a dwarf entertainer jousting slave who never even meets Dany. I realize he picks up Jorah Mormont and gains perspective from Penny, but seriously this should not take this long. Does Penny actually serve a purpose? Quentyn goes on his own journey, reaches Meereen, and essentially does completely nothing. There is no interesting dialogue between him and Dany or anyone else really, and the only thing he does is free the two dragons. Dany's POV's are basically her being too nice/nave/little lustful girl and failing as a ruler. All of them. I felt they were extremely repetitive and worse, destroyed the \\"great characters with vibrant personalities whose actions made sense within the context of those personalities\\" aspect of the series. How does she turn into a complete wise-beyond-her-years badass with the strength to lead a broken khalasar who frees Unsullied to a super lustful teenage girl? I don't buy the regression. You can say she's just a girl, she's Sansa with dragons, but that's not true. She was so much stronger and decisive and commanding and she suddenly self-implodes while not moving the plot along whatsoever? Come on.<br /><br />The problem is the events in this story arc don't overlap. You could read one characters POV through and essentially just get their own story. Contrast that with my examples from A Game of Thrones, where each different character's POV built upon each other and moved the story along. Overall, in Dance, there are many things that do not fit and do not tie together cohesively. You randomly get Jamie's chapter with a Brienne cameo, which was awesome, but then nothing happens. You random get a chapter with Aero Hotah to see a glimpse of Doran Martell, which was again awesome, but then nothing happens. These are tremendously written chapters, but what is the point of having them here and then making no mention of them until the next book five years down the line? Arya's chapters are the same thing. These chapters seem like cameos (and are some of my favorites) to let fans read about the characters without actually doing a single thing to progress the plot. There is so much happening everywhere and nothing is related. All of a sudden, I go read about Arya in Braavos to Quentyn on the way to Meereen to Jon on the wall to Jaime in the Riverlands. Nothing ties together or works together the way it did in Game of Thrones. Many characters have tremendously different goals then the realm, which is the thing that tied everything together in the first book. Now we have Dany sitting in Meereen for basically no discernible reason, and many readers have spoken out, \\"we don't care about Essos.\\" You can't start out a series with a focus on the throne in Westeros and then spend such significant time in Essos but accomplish absolutely nothing there.<br /><br />I feel like the series has gotten far away from the tightly woven narrative structure that was so fascinating to read from so many different characters' POV's. Now, we have even more and different characters' POV's, but they each tell different stories that are not inter-woven and are unfocused. It makes it hard to get through when nothing relates to anything else.<br /><br />The book's saving grace is the Northern storyline told mainly through Davos and Theon, with a little bit of Jon. I am not surprised at all these are many people's favorite chapters; it is because they follow the same formula as A Game of Thrones. Davos is \\"killed\\" by Manderly, but he is not and instead must go to Skagos. Huge awesome event that moves the plot. At the same time, we learn that Manderly is still very much committed to the Starks and hates the Freys and Boltons. This information is crucial to reading the later Theon chapters, since we now know exactly where Manderly's loyalties lie. Likewise, Jon sends Mance to Winterfell, and we meet Abel and his spearwives. The banker from the Iron Bank helps Jon, and then is crucial to saving Asha's men and bringing Theon and fake Arya to Stannis. One thing in one character's POV comes up again in the others, giving the story arc continuity and logical flow. This does not happen in the other story arcs, which are essentially just filler. I was honestly bored reading some of it, and that is something I would never have expected in reading this series.<br /><br />I will, of course, be buying and reading every book with just as much fervor as they come out. LOSING TIME - GRAND FINALE    This here is the album's infamous \\"Epic\\" title track, and Dream Theater's, by far, longest song, clocking in at an honest-to-God 42:04. The song, which has 8 portions to it, is actually broken into 8 tracks. Let me tell you all right now that this song is in-freaking-credible. I, seriously, cannot find the right words to describe how amazing it is. I don't think I'll ever be able to describe how awesome this song really is.    The entire song talks about 6 different people (hense the title, \\"Six Degrees Of Inner Turbulence\\"), who each suffer from a form of mental illness.     I. \\"Overture\\" serves as the song's instrumental introduction, and what an amazing intro it is. This would be TRACK #1 for DISC #2.    II. \\"About To Crash\\" talks about a young girl suffering from \\"Bipolar Disorder\\". The lyrics suggest that this part of the song is being told from the point of view of, either, the girl's father, or the girl's boyfriend. Tjis would be TRACK #2  for DISC #2.    III. \\"War Inside My Head\\" talks about man, who had fought in Vietnam, many years ago, and is now suffering from \\"Post-Traumatic Stress Disorder\\". With this illness, the man in the story believes he is still out in Vietnam. This would be TRACK #3  for DISC #2.    IV. \\"The Test That Stumped Them All\\" talks about a man, who suffers from \\"Schizophrenia\\". The doctors in this story discover that there is no cure for \\"Schizophrenia\\" and that there is little information out there. This would be TRACK #4 for DISC #2.    V. \\"Goodnight Kiss\\" talks about a woman, who has lost her child, in some way or form, and is, now, suffering from \\"Post-Partum Depression\\". It becomes somewhat apparent that her child has been hospitalized, or may have passed away at birth. This would be TRACK #5 for DISC #2.    VI. \\"Solitary Shell\\" tells of a young man suffering from \\"Autism\\". The story reveals that he had started off quite normal. But some years later, he had become, suddenly, withdrawn from all social contact. This would be TRACK #6 for DISC #2.    VII. \\"About To Crash\\" (Reprise) goes back to our first patient: the young girl, who suffers from \\"Bipolar Disorder\\". Only, this time, this part of her story is being given from the patient's point of view. This would be TRACK #7 for DISC #2.    VIII. \\"Losing Time\\" / \\"Grand Finale\\" ... The first part of this story, \\"Losing Time\\", tells of a girl suffering from \\"Dissociative Identity Disorder\\", previously known as \\"Multiple Personality Disorder\\". It explains that she has little to no friends, most likely due to this girl having multiple personalities throughout her life. This disorder causes the girl to have a result of amnesia, where she cannot keep track of her life among her many personalities, hence the title, \\"Losing Time\\". \\"The Grand Finale\\", which isthe second part of this story, informs us that we have to be more understanding of all people, who suffer from these disorders, and not to be heartless towards them, while also giving a short overview of each disorder. This would be TRACK #8 for DISC #2.       This whole album, as I have stated, is simply amazing. I have also mentioned that Dream Theater has become quite known for their lengthy \\"Epic\\" songs. \\"A MIND BESIDE ITSELF\\" (despite being split into 3 tracks) clocks in at 20:26. \\"A CHANGE OF SEASONS\\" clocks in at 23:06. With this album, \\"Six Degrees Of Inner Turbulence\\" (despite being split into 8 tracks) clocks in at a lengthy 42:04. If you are a Dream Theater fan, then you have to have this album in your collection, especially if you are a hardcore fan.

B000021XS0|5.0|\\"Metropolis Pt. 2: Scenes From A Memory\\" is Dream Theater's eighth album. It is also an instant masterpiece.    An interesting thing about this album is that during the recording sessions for 1997's \\"Falling Into Infinity\\", Dream Theater had written a 20+ minuite sequel to \\"Metropolis Pt. 1: The Miracle And The Sleeper\\". That sequel was, obviously, \\"Metropolis Pt. 2: Scenes From A Memory\\". But the producer that was working with Dream Theater during the \\"Falling Into Infinity\\" sessions deemed the track too long to include, as \\"Falling Into Infinity\\" would've been a double album. Ever since then, Dream Theater decided that they would work with outside producers no more. (Guitarist) John Petrucci and (Drummer) Mike Portnoy would go on to self-produce the albums themselves, starting with this album.     They resumed working on \\"Metropolis Pt. 2: Scenes From A Memory\\", where they decided to break it up into nine different songs, where the album would become a concept album.     The album's story is what makes the album more awesome than it already is. It tells the story of a young man named Nicholas. Nicholas is having this recurring dream about a young girl named Victoria Page, who was shot and killed in 1928. To figure out what this dream means, Nicholas goes to a hypnotherapist. While under hypnosis, Nicholas discovers that HE was Victoria Page in a past life, and that his recurring dream is ACTUALLY a past life memory.     Now, what I just described is the basic jidst of the story. Any and all details were left out, which means you will have to listen to the whole album to put the details together, piecing the whole story together for yourselves.    \\"SCENE ONE\\": REGRESSION    The best way to start off this album. I can't think of any other way to start of this album that is better than this opening track.    \\"SCENE TWO\\": I. OVERTURE 1928    Awesome.    \\"SCENE TWO\\": II. STRANGE DEJA VU    The best way to end \\"Scene Two\\".    \\"SCENE THREE\\": I. THROUGH MY WORDS    Amazing.    \\"SCENE THREE\\": II. FATAL TRAGEDY    Incredible.    \\"SCENE FOUR\\": BEYOND THIS LIFE    Loved every minute of this track.    \\"SCENE FIVE\\": THROUGH HER EYES    An awesome track, featuring additional vocals by gospel singer Theresa Thomason. Theresa has such a beautiful voice and it should be noted that she most definitely deserved to weave her voice right next to James' incredible voice.    \\"SCENE SIX\\": HOME    Awesome.    \\"SCENE SEVEN\\": I. THE DANCE OF ETERNITY    Awesme way to start off \\"Scene Seven\\".    \\"SCENE SEVEN\\": II. ONE LAST TIME    Incredible, once again.    \\"SCENE EIGHT\\": THE SPIRIT CARRIES ON    My favorite song from this album. Once again, Theresa Thomason gets to weave her beautiful voice next to James' awesome voice.    \\"SCENE NINE\\": FINALLY FREE    Incredible way to end this album.    This album, as I said before, is a brilliant masterpiece. It is definitely another one of my favorite Dream Theater albums.    NOTE: This album marks the first album to feature Dream Theater's third keyboardist ... Jordan Rudess, the keyboard wizard.     Jordan was originally asked to join Dream Theater back in 1994, as a replacement for Kevin Moore. He passed the audition with flying colors, but Jordan ultimately decided to tour with The Dixie Dreggs instead, due to the flexibility. That is actually how Derek Sherinian became Kevin Moore's replacement.    In 1997 or 1998, when Mike Portnoy, through the request of Magna Carta Records, was finally able to get his side band, LIQUID TENSION EXPERIMENT, off the ground. Mike brought in bassist Tony Levin, Jordan Rudess (to, obviously, play keyboards), and fellow Dream Theater pal, John Petrucci to play guitars.     After playing with Jordan for a year, John P., and Mike asked Jordan, for a second time, if he wanted to join Dream Theater. He accepted, where Dream Theater had to let Derek go, to make way for Jordan. Derek claims that, although sad at first at their decision, he said it was for the best, as he got a chance toembark on a solo career, as well as form his own band, PLANET X. Derek also claims (according to what I have read) that he is still good friends with Dream Theater.

The story behind this song is just purely amazing:    I. \\"Someone Like Him\\"    This part of the song tells of a man who decides on he wants to do in his life, not wanting to become an ordinary person. He succeeds in getting the extraordinary life he's always wanted. Soon after he gets the lifehe wants, he winds reflecting on it, and realizes that he really did not want to become successful, rather wanting to become an ordinary person after all. This song also displays the album's overall theme of everything coming full circle: Ending up where you originally began.    II. \\"Medicate (Awakening)\\"    In this part of the song, a man wakes up in a hospital and finds a doctor sitting at his bedside. This doctor informs the man that he had been in a catatonic sleep for, believe it or not, 30 years. The doctor is convinced that this man is cured. Suddenly, the patient is in extreme need of help, as he begins to feel that his consciousness is fading. The doctor tries everything he can, including prescribing a higher dosage of his medication, but it will not help. Despite the doctor's apparent failure, the patient informs the doctor that he is not at fault, and that he should not be ashamed. Soon after, the patient slips back into unconsciousness. Once again, the album's \\"Full Circle\\" theme is evident here.    III. \\"Full Circle\\"    This part of the song is actually (Drummer) Mike Portnoy's homage to the progressive rock scene. The lyrics in this part of the song display several references to his favorite songs, bands, and many others.    IV. \\"Intervals\\"    This part of the song is pretty much an overview of the album's songlist. Before each stanza in this part of the song's lyrics, (Drummer) Mike Portnoy says a scale degree.     V. \\"Razor's Edge\\"    This is the final part of the song, where it clearly emphasizes the cyclical nature of all things, especially where it ends.    This entire album was just way to awesome for me to describe. It has become another one of my all-time favorite Dream Theater albums. It is an album that everyone must have.

0553805371|4.0|Stephen Hawking is the world's most famous scientist. His accomplishment in theoretical physics are fairly obscure, but he well-known for surviving a degenerative nerve disease that crippled his body, and for writing one of the best-selling science books ever written.    Hawking's 1988 [[ASIN:0553380168 A Brief History of Time]] sold ten million copies. The book was not an easy read, and it is a wonder that is sold so well. Now Hawking has teamed up with Leonard Mlodinow to write a more readable and more current book on physics and cosmology.    The Grand Design give a short and entertaining history of astronomy and physics. It explains how ancient Greeks theorized about atoms, musical harmonies, solar eclipses, and planetary orbits. Most of all, they used observation and mathematics to systematically explain the natural world. That story continues into the 20th century, with the discoveries of relativity and quantum mechanics.    The authors attempt to address the question of reality. When strange properties of atoms and collapsed stars were discovered, the question naturally arises as to whether they are as real as everyday objects. The question is particularly difficult when there are multiple theories that are consistent with the observational data, such as the Ptolemy and Copernicus models of the solar system. Modern relativity teaches that these are equally valid, so it is not clear whether it is correct to say that the Earth really moves.    The book is an extended argument for scientific determinism and the reality of mathematical models. The view is that if a mathematical model is attractive enough, then we should adopt it as being real. If there is an aesthetically pleasing model of multiple universes that physicists adopt, then we should accept those universes as being real, even if there is no hope of ever observing them.    While Hawking's earlier book attempted to gain knowledge about the mind of God, this book declares God to be unnecessary, and so istheology and philosophy. Modern mathematical theories of physics can explain it all. God is not even needed to create the big bang that started the universe, as physics can explain why the big bang happened.    The climax of the book is to push an esoteric theory called M-theory, and formerly called string theory. They don't really tell us what M-theory is, or what the evidence for it is. They do say that it is the culmination of Einstein's vision of a unified theory of everything. It is not a scientific theory in the traditional sense of explaining observations. It is a loose collection of ideas centered around the conviction that all questions can be answered by pure thought from theoretical physicists who believe in the reality of mathematical models.

B004ZG5EYC|4.0|The movie:<br /><br />I won't belabor the point. Either you \\"get it\\" or you don't. The movie is loaded with emotion, charm, mystery, excitement, and quiet beauty. It's the story of an unusual French woman who is trying to find happiness, while not losing her quirky nature. Amelie is utterly charming and engrossing, due in part to the performance of the lead, Audrey Tautou, but also due to the really spectacularly beautiful cinematography, color palette, and shot compositions. It's in French, and that's the only audio track on this disc. So know that you will have to read subtitles. But it's worth it.<br /><br />The Blu-Ray:<br /><br />OK. What do we want in order to induce us to buy a movie on Blu-Ray that we already own on DVD? In my book, there are three things:<br /><br />1. A reasonable price<br />2. All of the extras from the previous release<br />3. A video transfer that offers an easily appreciable improvement.<br /><br />Long review short, Amelie on Blu-Ray delivers on all three counts. Encoded onto a single BD-50 disc, this release contains a 1080p transfer of the film itself, and all special features. The special features are all in standard definition, 4:3. This is a disappointment, but is par for the course on a catalog re-release like this. The point is, they're all here, so you can ditch your DVD.<br /><br />The video transfer is truly wondrous. Detail is through the roof, displaying facial pores, cloth textures, and individual hairs that just were not evident on the DVD. The amped-up, slightly hot color scheme from the DVD and theatrical presentations is preserved accurately. There are no compression artifacts or color banding evident at any point. There appears to be no visible edge enhancement, and no digital noise reduction to interfere with detail. Blacks are dark and solid, but not crushed. If your brightness and gamma are set properly, you will be rewarded with loads of detail near black, especially on Tautou's dark hair.<br /><br />The audio sounds pretty much like the same DTS track from the DVD. I did not notice any changes or improvements, but then, I'm not much of an audiophile. It is a dialogue heavy movie, with atmospheric surrounds only at key moments. I will say that Tatou's performance is very softspoken, so sometimes the balance between these moments required more fiddling than I would like. But it's not awful.<br /><br />OK - so why am I giving this 4 stars instead of 5? My main gripe is the 14 full minutes of preview material at the front end of the disc - which cannot be skipped by pressing \\"Top Menu.\\" Instead, you have to manually advance through each stinking one, every single time you want to play the disc. There is a rudimentary \\"resume\\" feature, but this only saves your bacon if you failed to watch all the way through on your previous viewing, and if your player has continuous power and storage. Whenever you pop the disc in for a fresh viewing, here come the previews.<br /><br />It never ceases to amaze me how certain companies, and Lionsgate/Miramax is chief among them, punish their customers for purchasing their movies. I can't stand it, and it sours an otherwise lovely experience.<br /><br />My other gripe, which is more minor, is the paucity of subtitle options. On a 50gb disc, only having English and Spanish subs available is pretty lame. My wife had wanted a disc with French subtitles, so she could listen along with the French also being displayed on the screen. At least the titles aren't \\"burned\\" onto the transfer, meaning you can turn them off if you like. For those with constant-image-height front projection screens, you will be happy to know that the titles are entirely within the image field, not the letterbox bars.<br /><br />Conclusion:<br /><br />If you love this movie and have a Blu-Ray setup, it's hard to argue against this package. Cheap, beautiful, and comprehensive, it delivers on all fronts. If it weren't sabotaged by irritating decisions on the part of marketing boobs, it would be five stars all the way. As it is, it's still a must-buy for fans.

He went on to participate in the invasion of Okinawa, and finished his service as part of the US occupation forces in China.<br /><br />Sledge's account provides a rare glimpse into what it was like to be an enlisted man fighting in the ground war in the Pacific.  He does an outstanding job of describing the bonds that form between fighting men, the way they come to completely depend on each other and the bitter anguish of loss they feel when their friends die.<br /><br />While this book does definitely fall into the genre of military history, it also fits into that of biography, and psychology of war.  Sledge does not wax poetic, but is able to explain in clear, simple prose what it was like for him. Then he tells us about how he developed and applied his system, which caused his sales to take off. However, as the author states (and he sounds very sincere), he would have paid $10,000 for these ideas when he was in low-sales limbo. If you are also in this sales netherworld or want to avoid ending up there, this book may give you the framework that you need.
0440539811|4.0|This was the first non-linear book that I recall reading, back in the early 90s.  Wilson's ideas about the illusory nature of reality (which, in his view, is what we make of it), as well as his tongue-in-cheek portrayal of popular conspiracies, fired up my imagination and fuelled a lot of my own creative writing in the years since.  I've re-read the trilogy several times, and each time I do an interesting quote, idea, or piece of dialogue jumps out at me that I hadn't noticed before.  So trust me when I say that if you liked reading it once, you should enjoy it even more the second time.    While I don't necessarily agree with Wilson's libertarian worldview (which is plastered amusingly throughout the book), he does have important things to say about the need to be more skeptical about what we see and hear, and to understand that our own views of reality are just as skewed as everyone else's.  Hagbard Celine (who I interpret as Wilson himself, to a large degree) and Simon Moon are the most vocal characters in my view, and it is through them that the reader gets exposed to these ideas.    Wilson and Shea very cleverly weave historical events into the twisting plot, and they do a good job of capturing the overall feel of the countercultural movement of the 60s and early 70s - not surprising, given that they were both deeply involved in it.  What you get from this is a book that (on the surface) has a far-fetched plot, but if you read a bit more deeply and look up some of the real-world figures, historical events, and organisations, you will find that most of them really did, or do, exist.  This might lead you to question your initial skepticism about the storyline briefly, and in the end you will probably have questioned how you perceive the world - this is exactly what Wilson and Shea want you to do, as they explain in the appendices.    I would advise readers to think about this book not only as a convoluted, highly entertaining story, but also as an introductory guidebook on how step outside oneself and consider one's own beliefs and preconceptions about the world and how one interacts with it.    Geoff Gander, author of [[ASIN:B005202EMU The Tunnelers]]

0756405890|5.0|The Name of the Wind is the first book in the Kingkiller Chronicles written by Patrick Rothfuss. Name of the Wind is in the fantasy genre and the author's first published novel. The book is about an innkeeper, named Kvothe, who tells a scribe his life story. Kvothe relates how he developed from an orphan to a legend. The narrative is rich in foreshadowing and plot twists. The heartbeat of The Name of the Wind is its heavy reliance on poetry and music. In fact, an element of particular enjoyment for me was how the book was written almost like a musical composition consisting of highs and lows; tragedy and despair followed by adoration and praise. It feels like a classical piece could easily be written to encapsulate The Name of the Wind. Rothfuss' work constantly brings a reader through passages of great pain and great joy. All of this is felt through the eyes of young Kvothe. Many books have similar themes and characters but what really brings the book together is Rothfuss' musically based pacing. Some chapters are 20 pages long while others are a few paragraphs. The book has a dance-like quality as it flips back and forth between biographical narration from Kvothe to the scribe and the story being provided. These elements create drama and insight into the story being told.<br /><br />Rothfuss breaks recent fantasy norms and writes a book basically concerning one character's point of view. Kvothe is a<br />fascinating part of the book that keeps the pages turning. This character is brilliant, musically talented, and determined. However, I can honestly say after reading the first book I do not understand him any more than his girlfriend Denna.<br />Does Kvothe have a streak of greatness in him like a Napoleon or an Alexander the Great? Is he \\"special\\" like Harry Potter?<br />What makes him such an incredible character? Are brains, determination, and luck enough to make a man like this? The Name of the Wind largely leaves these questions unanswered.<br /><br />Areas of weakness:<br />Young Kvothe's story often times feels like it could be the next chapter in A Series of Misfortunate Events. Without fail, after a passage of great success in the life of young Kvothe, a great tragedy would soon befall him. It is understandable why Rothfuss chooses to do this as it adds depth to the character and brings a natural audience support, but all of the misery does get a bit tiring.<br /><br />Young Kvothe also develops a strong arrogant streak in the middle of the book which is wearisome to read. The flaw adds depth to his character but is a bother to read through.<br />The supporting cast in The Name of the Wind also were, overall, bland. Kvothe's school yard nemesis, Amrbose, was a typical rich bully who constantly fought with Kvothe. He would use typical cheap tactics to try to increase Kvothe's misery and most of the time they worked.<br /><br />Kvothe's closest friends were the typical mates who never failed to forgive Kvothe for any transgression, always were partial to sharing a drink, and talked about how Kvothe just couldn't understand the ladies. As a side note, Rothfuss' Wisconsin roots were obvious throughout the text of the book as it contained a large amount conversations concerning alcohol.<br />Despite the weaknesses, Rothfuss has written a very good story that deserves a read. If you enjoyed Harry Potter you will find an pleasing summer read in the pages of the Name of the Wind.story often times feels like it could be the next chapter in A Series of Misfortunate Events. B005DC68NI|5.0|Let's Get Digital: How to Self-Publish, And Why You Should is a new book by David Gaughran that describes and documents the processes any writer, be they old or new, needs to know to become a self-published phenomenon. The book is divided into three sections, each dealing with a different aspect of self-publishing, including: Digital Revolution, Digital Self-publishing, and Success Stories, then ends with some additional topics to completely round out the informational package.<br /><br />In Digital Revolution, the author does a fine job of walking readers through the major forces driving publishing into the digital age with e-books and why you, as a writer should consider it when it comes time to releasing your own work. If you're been following any site where conversations about self-publishing are going on, then none of the topics will be new to you. However, seeing them in one place certainly provides the new writer with all the information on the new digital publishing world for them to make a decision. David doesn't recommend any particular solution, leaving that up to you, but he does emphasize, as do many others that you should do what's best for you at the time. What's best for you will certainly change over time as the industry is far from settled yet; so being flexible is one of the major traits to practice.<br /><br />In Digital Self-publishing, you're walked though a series of reproducible steps that anyone can use to get their work into digital bookstores. Each section provides a high-level goal that writers will need to address at some point in order to turn their writing efforts into a successful product. David emphasizes writing the best story you can, designing great covers, editing, formatting, pricing, publicizing, handling reviews, and other means of getting the word out there to make your book just as professional and successful as possible. As the market is changing so fast, providing hard and fast rules that might be outdated in a few months would make no sense, but if you can follow the general principles outlined in these chapters you'll be one step ahead of the game.<br /><br />Next up are the Success Stories, 33 authors who'd entered the digital battlefield and done well, including Consuelo Saah Baehr, Terri Reid, N. Gemini Sasson, Katie Klein, and many more. Written in their own words it's a nice cross-section of stories that show how anyone of courage, some business sense, and a desire to publish their work can triumph in online ebook sales. I found this to be the best section of the book, simply because it had real authors with great stories about how they came to self-publishing. Most simply couldn't get their work to the public via the big publisher route through no fault of their own. Instead, most came hesitantly into self-publishing and have done better than they ever imagined, in the positive feedback from thousands of new readers, to monetary, and critical success. To know so many have successfully followed the self-publishing path is encouraging and inspirational.<br /><br />The author also ends with some advice on the resurgence of short stories for busy readers, accessing the international market with your work, some practical considerations to keep in mind like copyrights, foreign sales, and a host of resource links to aid you in finding help on your way to self-publishing success, from editing, to covers, to publicizing, to well, almost anything a writer may need help with.<br /><br />If you're a new writer, or an old writer looking into this new fangled self-publishing thing, having all this information in one place can save you hours of web surfing time. One thing I hope David addresses is to add or update the information in this guide as new information becomes available. The publishing industry is changing and the processes and methods that work today may not tomorrow. Also, be sure to check out David's blog, davidgaughran.wordpress.com where he covers the changing self-publishinglandscape often and in detail.

It is quite simply what music should be, a strong contemporary sound that is firmly grounded in the past. Every lick says \\"I remember, but look at me now\\"...

B005DC68NI|5.0|The author takes a decided stand writing an instructional book about self-publishing, and in fact puts his work out there on trial for readers to determine for themselves whether it's coherent, organized, and professional enough to stand up to its own claims.    And not only does it stand up to those claims, it delivers a remarkable example of how to do it right.    Some self-help type books I've read muddle through the topic without offering really anything of substance.  In the end, you as the reader are given very little more than a couple of tidbits of good information and a load of repetitive tips and advice, most of which you already know.  Not so here.  Mr. Gaughran is not only succinct and to the point, he offers a very critical look at all facets of the publishing industry of today, and makes some rather keen observations on where it is headed in light of the emergence of the e-book.    He then focuses on specific areas of concern to authors trying to self-publish, and gives clear reasons for the advice.  His writing is very clear and understandable, and does not belabor the point too much before moving on to the next one.    He has also included a very interesting section of the book with a snapshot of thirty-plus authors who have had varying degrees of success self-publishing e-books.  Very candid looks at people who have succeeded using the exact advice he gives us in this book.    Well worth the price!

B0000D96NW|5.0|Machine Head's fifth studio album Through The Ashes Of Empires was released in 2003 to a mixture of praise from some fans and suspicion from others.<br /><br />The album was a lot heavier and harder than the two albums which had preceded it, but all the same was not a wholesale return to the style found on the band's first two albums. Some fans of heavier metal may have still been unsure about all the softer moments and mid tempo sections and decided not to give the album a fair chance.<br /><br />Through The Ashes Of Empires maintained the clean vocals and melodic tendencies which were present on the more recent albums at the time, mixed them with some of the heaviness and groove from the early days of their career and came up with something modern, interesting and enjoyable.<br /><br />The album has three songs which exceed seven minutes in length and only two which run under the four minute margin. Rapping is altogether absent from the vocal style and there is no industrial or electronic influence to the music or production whatsoever.<br /><br />The band would later develop and perfect this style on their next album, The Blackening, which mixed in huge deals of dual guitar work and thrash influenced riffs to create one of their most popular and respected works to date.<br /><br />Early fans who were won back by The Blackening should take time to give Through The Ashes Of Empires a fair listen, and may find a great album they have missed out on.<br /><br />Standout tracks include the opener `Imperium,' in addition to heavier numbers like `Vim,' and `Wipe The Tears.' Furthermore, additional praise is deserved for the intense and brooding album closer `Descend The Shades Of Night,' which starts in a soft melodic fashion and rises to a dynamic climax.<br /><br />Overall, Through The Ashes Of Empires is one of Machine Head's better albums and fans who overlooked it at the time should reinvestigate it. For newcomers to the band, it would provide an excellent starting point as it bridges the gap between all of the band's directions.rections.

B005DC68NI|5.0|Given the speed with which electronic readers have proliferated, evolved, and, yes, disrupted the traditional models of publishing, writers who have attempted to shift along with the paradigm -- or ahead of it, in some cases -- have had to hopscotch between message boards, blogs, websites, and their own experimentation to assemble the how-to's.    Let's Get Digital changes that.    The book begins with a conversational, yet comprehensive, account of the current shift from paper to electronic books, and the implications for publishers, editors, bookstores, readers, and writers. It is here that Mr. Gaughran makes his compelling argument for why the trend is likely to accelerate, and why writers stand to benefit the most. I found the section fascinating, not only from an author's perspective, but also for the thoroughness with which it chronicles what is perhaps the most disruptive innovation in publishing since the printing press -- and as it's happening. In this way, Let's Get Digital becomes its own testament to the trend.                  The next section, however, is where Let's Get Digital shines: a logical, step-wise guide of the how-to's. How to write, design, edit, format, upload, and promote one's work. All in one place. No more hopscotching. As one who recently uploaded his first title, I found myself nodding my head in agreement with many of Mr. Gaughran's instructions and insights, and scribbling down many others that I had not yet considered, particularly those pertaining to promotion. This section, as well a special appendix, contains clickable links to the many resources he recommends. In addition to scribbling, I created a folder on my desktop and filled it with bookmarks.    The final section, the success stories, is the fun icing on the cake. It can be read all at once, or dipped into a few stories at a time for inspiration (as I did). If I have any criticisms here it would be that a few of the authors speak of their success without quite fleshing out the steps they took to attain it -- the very thing inquiring newbies want to know. But this is minor quibble that does nothing to detract from the overall intelligence and utility of the book.

B002ZG980U|1.0|Leonardo DiCaprio plays Dom Cobb, a thief specializing in corporate espionage. Cobb has an unusual modus operandi: he takes his victims by surprise on trains and airplanes, drugs them with powerful sedatives, hooks them up to portable dream-reading machines, and then violates their minds to extract deeply hidden secrets. Cobb sells these secrets for heaps of cash to his corporate sponsors. Assisting Cobb in these exploits is a crew of crooks that includes a forger, a weapons specialist, a drug peddler, and a cute college coed.     When Cobb is not dream-jacking his victims he is pining after his two children whom he can no longer visit. You see, we are meant to sympathize with Cobb because an inception gone wrong resulted in the tragic suicide of his wife, Mal. Cobb was unjustly accused of murdering Mal and he begins the film a fugitive. To make matters worse, Cobb's guilty subconscious is haunted by the projection of a vindictive murderous Mal.     Subtle clues are hidden throughout the film that Cobb's dreams and reality may not be as distinguishable as he thinks. It is the interpretation of these clues that intelligent movie-goers will enjoy debating. For example, in one nuanced flashback Cobb's wife tells him, \\"Our world isn't real.\\" To prove her point she commits suicide by jumping off a ledge... on their wedding anniversary.    My chief complaint with Inception is not the film's faux intelligence but the depressing, humorless melodrama. A lighter more upbeat tone would have better suited this film's preposterous story line. In my opinion, The Truman Show covers similar ground more ably and with comedic flair. The Truman show cleverly explores the concept of inception (i.e., covertly planting the seed of an idea within a person's mind) through the exploitation of Truman's relationships with his father, wife, and best friend. The Truman Show rightly places the psychological elements at the forefront. Inception, on the other hand, is centered around gratuitous gunfights, car chases, and explosions.     I rate Inception one star because I could barely sit through the entire movie even once. Surely, I will never view this movie again. If there is a sequel I will not view that either. Even my enthusiasm for further Batman films has suffered. I do not recommend Inception.

B00000AF98|2.0|1987's Crazy Nights is far from being the best KISS album, in fact some of the band's other 1980's work is stronger and more consistent than this. Yet Crazy Nights is not a bad album, it sees KISS going further down the pop route and the album's direction is similar to what a lot of other bands were doing at the time in the Glam/Hair scene. I truly appreciate Crazy Nights for what it is: a good pop album. If you don't like the sound of Hair/Glam/Pop Metal then the album won't have any appeal to you. It doesn't hold up as well over time, it's one of those weaker but enjoyable KISS albums that its moments. To be honest some of the songs sound dated and some can't be classified as much more than filler or throw away material. It's a fun album but it lacked what made KISS unique and interesting in the first place, here the band sounds blank and just like every other Glam/Hair band at the time.<br /><br />Paul Stanley said he wrote a lot of the album's content on Keyboards which can be heard easily throughout Crazy Nights, there's a heavy use of Keyboards which was commonly found in music at the time. Gene Simmons doesn't deliver his best 80's material here but \\"Good Girl Gone Bad\\" is fun and \\"Hell Or High Water\\" has its place on the album (still much better lyrics than \\"Burn Bitch Burn\\" with \\"I wanna put my log in your fireplace\\"...). Guitarist Bruce Kulick once again did some good work (listen to the shredding on No No No, this is a guitarist that no doubt adapted very well with what was current)fortunately Eric Carr gets little room to display his talent behind the drums.<br /><br />The title track is a very pop effort and fits perfectly with what was popular at the time. It's perhaps a little too happy but it's undeniably catchy and it was a hit for band, particularly in Europe. \\"I'll Fight Hell To Hold You\\" is generally not liked by many reviewers but I think it's actually one of the strongest tracks of the album. Its big riff and Paul's vocals on this one make it a much better song than given credit for. \\"Bang Bang You\\" may have been a stronger song back then but today it's considered cheesy and dated. \\"No No No\\" is much of the same and has much more in common with bands like Danger Danger than the KISS people usually remember. \\"Hell or High Water\\"\\"Reason To Live\\" is a great ballad and even if it's not one of the best KISS ballads it's a powerful and touching one that I quite like. \\"My Way\\" is more interesting and is very melodic, note the heavy use of keyboards here. \\"Good Girl Gone Bad\\" sees Gene doing some very tong-in-cheek songwriting (you know you like it). This track is a guilty pleasure, its dirty, catchy, and features great guitar playing from Bruce Kulick.<br /><br />Crazy Nights is not one of my favorite KISS albums, in fact it's probably one of the weaker releases. Blame it on Ron Nevison's , say it was overproduced or blame it on the group's tendency to follow what was popular at the time but it's just a very good album. No doubt it was cutting edge at the time but a lot of the music simply doesn't hold up that well after all these years. Crazy Nights unlike some of KISS' timeless material is very dated in its era and sort of serves as a time capsule of that time in KISS' already lengthy career. I quite like some of the tracks but there's just not enough good material to save it. As big a KISS fan as I am, I'll have to put my admiration of the band aside and be objective in my rating. I can't rate this album higher than 2 stars. It sounded too thin; it was too poppy and suffered from too much production. 2/5 stars.much better song than given credit for. \\"Bang Bang You\\" may have been a stronger song back then but today it's considered cheesy and dated. \\"No No No\\" is much of the same and has much more in common with bands like Danger Danger than the KISS people usually remember. \\"Hell or High Water\\"\\"Reason To Live\\" is a great ballad and even if it's not one of the best KISS ballads it's a powerful and touching one that I quite like. \\"My Way\\" is more interesting and is very melodic, note the heavy use of keyboards here. \\"Good Girl Gone Bad\\" sees Gene doing some very tong-in-cheek songwriting (you know you like it). B0032UYFA6|5.0|So many others have done such a comprehensive job on covering this season, let me focus on one episode to expose the brilliance of the writing on \\"Breaking Bad\\".     In EP 10, \\"Fly\\" (stop now if you don't want some spoilers) We see that Walt has become obsessed with cleanliness in the lab to the point of mania.  A single fly cannot be tolerated, and Walt forces Jesse to pursue the fly, even to the point of putting their \\"schedule\\" at risk.     Jesse soon begins to become perplexed by Walt's behavior, and finally decides to drug Walt's coffee so he can resume cooking.  It is at this point that Walt begins to speak in a free stream of conscious thought that he is normally too guarded to speak about.  Walt expresses the very incalculable odds that he would meet Jesse's girlfriend's father in a bar, and that he would go on to be the air traffic controller who would cause a jet crash which lead to the death of a huge number of people.  It is at this point that the viewer is on the edge of his seat wondering if Walt will tell Jesse that he basically stood by as Jesse's girfriend died in a heroin daze, but he doesn't, he simply says how sorry he is...    Walt goes on to express via that it is foreign to him to consider the odds of he and Jesse's actions.  He ponders the exact combination of words that would make his wife understand why he did what he did.  After all, if not for worrying about his family he would never have considered producing meth in the first place--he didn't want his family impoverished by his illness.    We see via this series that Walt is a man who is very precise in his actions, as would be expected of a genious in crystallography.  He cuts the crusts off his sandwiches, as if to make them perfectly square--and it is this precision that confuses him when things don't work out correctly.    But it is the very fact that Jesse and Walt's actions have wide ranging effects on others (such as the jet crash) that truly has shocked Walter White.  He is terrified by a \\"butterfly effect\\" by which he fears that in time Karma will impose his doom, and will take everyone with him.  Is the cost worth it?  Will all the money in the world bring back the dead?  What if his family is murdered?  All these effects are beyond Walter's control, and it is very uncomfortable to him.    So, in this episode, we see why the prescence of a single fly bothers Walter so much.  It is symbolic of his efforts to control his own destiny and those who he loves, but by the end of the episode Walter says, \\"Forget it, Jesse\\" as if understanding at last that so much is beyond his control.    This is why this show is brilliant, because so much can be said without spelling it out directly, all through the hunt for a single fly.

B000DZ6VAK|5.0|I agree with the many posters who praise these excellent performances. Uchida is a strong pianist with a fine Mozart touch, especially effective in the cadenzas. The English Chamber Orchestra under Jeffrey Tate provides lively rhythms most of the time, but is able to darken their tone appropriately where necessary, as in the 20th, the first in a minor key.<br /><br />(I feel a special relationship to that concerto. Many years ago, a friend of mine and I were spending a week at the BSO summer festival in Tanglewood. We came upon a piano in one of the sheds and noticed on the stand the music for the Mozart 20th in an edition for 4 hands. We had the nerve of the very young and sat down to try our hand (hands) on the slow middle movement. No one came to cart us away; instead, a man came along and turned pages for us. We played raggedly but with spirit. Anyway, that's what the man, who we recognized as Aaron Copland, told us when we finished. I can never hear this beautiful music without thinking of that extraordinary event.)<br /><br />Anyway, these are all fine performances, a worthy addition to any Mozart collection, indeed a perfectly good place to start if you don't already have other versions of these great works for piano and orchestra. Highly recommended.

The statement is absurd, and Dawkins chooses to ignore all the greatest leaders of religion, because they are facts about religion not convenient for his thesis.  As a scientist, he should know better than to ignore evidence simply because it is inconvenient to his thesis.  Second, (to subtract a second star) Dawkins makes the equally asinine claim that he is not \\"aware of atheists\\" (see p. 309-316) who persecuted others in the name of their ideology.  Really?!  Mao Zedong and Josef Stalin never existed?  And they never abused or killed religious person or shut down religious institutions because they opposed their atheism?  That statement is so dishonest it is hard to understand how an intelligent person can utter it.  Thirdly (subtract a third star) Dawkins method is badly slanted throughout.  Anyone familiar with the American \\"Founding Fathers\\" (he is fond of quoting Jefferson) knows they had many ambiguous (both positive and negative) statements about religion.  But typical to his slanted and disingenuous method, Dawkins quotes only the negative statements of religion from the \\"Founders\\", and in fact his whole method is only to drudge up the worst in religion and claim it represents all religion.  One might as equally say that all democracy is an evil, because the democratic United States was guilty of killing Indians and enslaving African Americans.  The logic is about as binding and compelling.  So if you have never read an atheistic book, you may learn some of the bad things religion has done, which may be worth your while.  Absolutist, science-avoiding religion is indeed stupid, but it doesn't represent all religion.  If you have read atheists books, however, or know even a fractional bit about religion you will see that Dawkins's book is vapid and hollow.  It is sad that such tripe wins uncritical acclaim.

B005DC68NI|5.0|First off, I was given a copy of this book to review prior to it being available here on Amazon. I have been researching self-publishing for several months, am a regular over at the Kindleboards, JA Konrath's blog and the myriad Twitter feeds that daily tweet new links. It's a rollercoaster ride keeping up with everything on a daily basis, especially when it can change so much from one day to the next.    That said, I've read David Gaughran's LET'S GET DIGITAL and can safely say this book will give, in a nutshell, any newbies or those casually interested in what self-publishing can offer today an excellent introduction. This is not necessarily a how-to book on getting your book onto Amazon's Kindle, B&N Nook, Smashwords or the myriad other outlets available to buy/sell ebooks (or print novels available through sites like CreateSpace or Lightening Source). No, this is an introduction (and a very thorough and well-written one at that) to the self-publishing industry itself, complete with simple math comparing current royalty rates, as well as a number of real life situations with authors who found success beyond their wildest dreams. Mr. Gaughran compares traditional publishing with self publishing and manages to stay fair on both sides, but does point out how the business math works better (in some cases) when you do this yourself.    The first nearly two-thirds of the book is the nitty-gritty of the industry itself, how and why it works. Mr. Gaughran does a good job of making the sometimes confusing marketplace seem simple, as well as filling in a lot of blanks and answering a lot of questions most self-publishers (and those of us who intend to one day do it) face daily from other interested parties. Most of it is not new to anyone who has done in-depth research on the subject; he names Konrath, Hocking, and Locke quite a bit but also mentions some people less known, such as Black, Rose and Lieske, people who make a very comfortable living doing what they love (writing). He also gives tips such as how to use social media (such as Twitter and its #hashtags) and networking (do you know what a blog tour is? You will by the end of the book) to market your releases.    The last part of the book is from authors themselves: people who have gone from nothing to not-quite-overnight sensations. Make no mistake: this route is difficult and will crush lesser men and women, but for those who perservere, who can remember to keep working through the bad reviews and perceived rejection of their stories, there is a bona fide business opportunity here. These stories serve as motivation and the hope for a light at the end of the tunnel.    Final verdict: the book is nothing new for someone who's keeping tabs on every marketing opportunity, every small change in the industry, every chance that presents itself. However, for those of you who are searching for a good guide on what to do and how to do it (and more importantly, WHY to do it), this book is an EXCELLENT source. I know a lot and was still surprised by what I found in here - reminders of ideas I'd forgotten (I really need to use Twitter #hashtags more often) and those who I still need reminding (like negative reviews are par for the course). Great book, great writing, great information on an industry that is still at the beginning of its boom.

0307272702|4.0|Gary Taubes has written extensively about nutrition, and our popular misconception (propagated by the government and a few health organizations) about what, and how much, we should be eating. In his tome \\"Good Calories, Bad Calories,\\" he exhaustively researched the fallacies and faulty science supporting the low-fat eating style (based upon consumption of carbohydrates), and its result on public health.<br /><br />In essence, \\"Why We Get Fat\\" is very much the same. If you've already read \\"Good Calories, Bad Calories,\\" it'll be a familiar re-hashing (though with more recent information, studies, and insight-) into the health issues surrounding the nutritional recommendations by our government and medical groups. Taubes, citing historical demonstration and contemporary examples, postulates that the present obesity epidemic is, in essence, resultant from widespread malnutrition: as a whole, people consume too few nutrient-dense foods, and over-consume non-filling, insulin-desensitizing carbohydrates (which do little to sate our bodies, and wreck havoc on our energy levels).<br /><br />Additionally, he punches holes (citing scientific white papers and well-respected research studies, rather than pop-science and daytime television doctors) in the \\"calories in/calories out\\" premise of weight loss. Starvation, he notes multiple times, doesn't work for weight loss, and it's untenable to expect it to lead to any long-lasting health change. The constant jump and decline of insulin results in an ongoing hormonal imbalance, which ultimately results in increased carbohydrate consumption and obesity. Taubes recommends to the reader, then, to load their diet with fats and proteins - while avoiding simple (and especially processed) carbohydrates. Much like Michael Pollanan, with his \\"In Defense of Food,\\" Tabues makes a strong and methodically argued case for eating whole foods: full-fat meats, unprocessed foods, and plenty of vegetables.<br /><br />Very well-researched and organized. This book is less a diet book than an expository work on the cause, and potential treatments, for widespread obesity. Taubes is focused and concise in his detailing, and does an excellent job guiding the reader towards compelling evidence that discredits the low fat/high carbohydrate dietary guidelines we've been so insistently fed.diet book than an expository work on the cause, and potential treatments, for widespread obesity. B000JJ4GW0|2.0|When this came out back in 2006, I caved and bought this for the two new tracks. I reviewed this record a few years ago and gave it a pretty glowing review. I now regret that -- this is my re-review. I am as big a U2 fan as you can get -- I have all of their albums, both best-ofs, and nearly every CD single on the market of theirs. It is safe to say that I am a fanatical U2 fan, not to mention I have seen them six times. So the two stars are NOT because I don't like U2.     No, the two stars are because of the greed of the record companies. I have always thought that U2 has needed a boxed set. Kind of like Bob Dylan's 'Biograph' and Jimi Hendrix's more recent 'West Coast Seattle Boy.' U2, like Dylan, has plenty of great b-sides and other assorted rarities that never seem to make the cut on their albums. So instead of rehashing a useless, pointless singles collection, why not gather up all of the CD singles on several discs and issue their b-sides (including the remixes) all on a 4 or 5 disc collection? It makes sense. U2 needs a 'Complete Singles' boxed set.    Instead, we are treated to 16 songs U2 fans have time and time again and two new (for 2006, anyway) songs. The first, a cover of the old punk tune 'The Saints Are Coming' features Berkeley punk rockers Green Day. Yes, this track rocks. You get flashes of Green Day and U2 mixed together on this. It's an odd fusion, but it works. I'm not much of a Green Day fan these days (personally, I really didn't like '21st Century Breakdown' -- loved 'American Idiot' and I still do, but '21st Century Breakdown' was such a disappointment to me -- but that's another review), but they absolutely smoke here. This is an odd mix of bands, yes, but amazingly, it works.    'Window in the Skies' would not sound the least bit out of place on 2004's 'How to Dismantle an Atomic Bomb,' if it had been recorded back then. This is a catchy tune, starting off with The Edge's trademark guitar chimes, and it escalates into a good pop rock anthem. This track is a great tune from U2, I just wish they would have included it on an album instead of this compilation -- it really is deserving of appearing on an album.    Now, the title is what frustrates me. Again, I go back to the box set theory. Wouldn't it make more sense to gather up ALL of U2's singles from 1980 to 2006 (when this compilation was released) instead of just 16 (18, counting the two new tracks which were singles)? Think about it. 'I Will Follow' is missing, 'Stay (Faraway, So Close!)' is missing, 'Staring at the Sun' is missing, 'Who's Gonna Ride Your Wild Horses' is missing, 'Hold Me, Thrill Me, Kiss Me, Kill Me' (from the 'Batman Forever' soundtrack) is missing, and 'Even Better Than the Real Thing' is missing. I haven't even *begun* to touch on their other singles. It's ridiculous. Why they released this fresh on the heals of 2002's 'The Best of 1990-2000' is beyond me. It seems pointless.    Are you listening, Interscope Records? U2 is in dire need of a box set ASAP. This band has simply released too much great material AND b-sides to not have a box set. 'U218 Singles' is only recommended for the ***very*** casual fan and the devoted fan, just for the two new tracks. In fact, if it weren't for 'Window in the Skies,' this is easily a one-star disc. This is record company greed at its finest.     So, while I doubt any record executive will read my review, I hope the record companies and U2 get their act together and release a box set. Yeah, yeah the deluxe editions and reissues are nice, but a box set would be, to quote one of their songs, \\"even better than the real thing.\\"     (By the way, the bonus DVD from Milan really is great -- I just wish there was more. It seems more like a sampling than anything. Maybe Interscope will release the complete DVD at a later date, but right now, this is the best we're going to get. Sigh.)    Recommended ONLY for the ****REALLY**** casual fans who have heard 'Beautiful Day' on the radio and want it forthe car or the office and for the fanatical fans like myself for the two new songs, which are admittedly incredible.

B000W7JWUA|4.0|Plenty of people have done an admirable job of explaining the games in their reviews, so this is instead an attempt at a comparison between a number of games, the pros and cons of each and which may suit different people best. The games in question are: Carcassonne, Settlers of Catan, Castle Panic, Smallworld, and Forbidden Island.     We have had Settlers of Catan and Carcassonne (with a number of expansion packs) for quite a few years now, and only recently added the other games above. We usually either play just as two adults, or with our two older children (age 9 and 8), and so our conclusions are based on how these games work in those settings. So here's what we've found:    Settlers of Catan  We got this around the same time as Carcassonne and initially just didn't latch onto it. Partly it's that it's supposed to be 3 players or more, and we often play as just two of us. Once we found online some instructions for playing as 2 players it came out more often, and as time's gone by it's become fairly 50-50 whether we play Settlers or Carcassonne on a quiet night in. The choice will usually depend on how much we want to think. With Settlers, you're always planning and calculating; with Carcassonne, you're taking it a card at a time.     Who should get it: Settlers is well-known as one of the great modern games. I'm not as sold on it as some people, and it takes quite a while to learn and feel comfortable with, but once you get the hang of it, it is an entertaining and enjoyable addition to a games collection. There are several 2-player rule variations out there if you need them and they work well (we found one that worked for us and we've stuck to it). But this isn't a game for kids; I would suspect not until they're 16 or so. Amongst other things, I think they'll find it too dull.    Carcassonne  This has been a favorite for years now, and everyone we've played it with has gone off to get it themselves. We usually play without farms because it then becomes less directly competitive and more sociable. Kids can play it, adults can play it, it's relaxed, it's fun and it's simple to learn. Here's one nice thing about it: you don't have to be constantly thinking and planning ahead. You don't know what card you're going to draw next time, so you just play one card at a time. You're encouraged to discuss where to put a card, and since you don't know what piece you're getting next, your comments to another player are usually pretty unbiased.    Who should get it: In my experience, pretty much anyone, except those who want ultra-competitive games. The first few expansion packs are also well worth getting, but don't bother with anything from Mayor onward.     Castle Panic  The kids love this one, again it's simple to learn and it has the added bonus of allowing them to get out their aggressive instincts and go postal on monsters! They don't like the `master slayer' option, but prefer just straight cooperative play. After the first few plays, I've found the basic game is too easy, and so we're experimenting with making it more challenging, such as starting with no walls, or drawing 3 monster cards at a time instead of 2. I think Castle Panic will become a game that we get out pretty regularly to play.    Who should get it: People with kids, who want to play cooperative games. Could be fun as a party game too!    Smallworld  While the kids have enjoyed playing this, I think their interest is starting to wane already. I suspect it will work better as a game with a group of adults, or when the kids are older. It has a lot going for it, especially the creative cards and board, but as others have noted - what's with the box for the tokens? Very poorly designed and adds unnecessary annoyance. Most of the time when playing we've found it's not too directly competitive, it's easier to attack lost tribes or declining races, so generally it doesn't get too personal!    Who should get it: I think this would make a fun addition to a games collection, but I don't think it would be a go-to game, especially with kids. The rules are more complicated to learn and explain than the other games, and this makes it hard to just sit down with new players and get on with a game. Having said that, we've enjoyed playing it , and I think it'll get pulled out every now and then over the years.    Forbidden Island  Although the kids would prefer Castle Panic, when we've played Forbidden Island (at my insistence!) they've thoroughly enjoyed it. As the island starts to collapse in a heap toward the end of the game, the tension levels rise and people are on the edge of their seats! The game always ends with voices rising in pitch and tension as cards get turned over - it's fun! It's a pure cooperative game, and that works well for us as a family - no one feels bad, we're all in it together. We're still using the `Normal' level of play, maybe we'll notch up a level soon!    Who should get it: If you like cooperative games, I think this is excellent to have. I love how easy it is to set different difficulty levels, and it's definitely the game that's had the most excited tension - Castle Panic has this at times, but not sustained (at least as the basic game). It doesn't have the whole monster thing going for it that Castle Panic does, and I think that's why the kids haven't latched onto it so quickly (kill trolls or wander round an island getting treasure - which is your average kid going to choose?) but I suspect that long-term it'll have more staying power.

The new restoration features the film in its full negative aspect ratio of 2.55:1, wider than both the prints shown at the film's original limited Technirama engagements in 2.20:1 and the CinemaScope-compatible reduction prints for general release at 2.35:1.    Film Backround: Sleeping Beauty is a 1959 animated film produced by Walt Disney and based on the fairy tale by Charles Perrault. The film spent nearly the entire decade of the 1950s in production: the story work began in 1951, voices were recorded in 1952, animation production took from 1953 until 1958, and the musical score, mostly based on Tchaikovsky's ballet of the same name, was recorded in 1957. Sleeping Beauty's production costs, which totaled $6 million, made it the most expensive Disney film up to that point, and over twice as expensive as each of the preceding three Disney animated features. Sleeping Beauty's successful reissues have made it the second most successful film released in 1959, second to Ben-Hur.

B000EHSVS2|5.0|\\"...I believe in the old and sound rule that an ounce of sweat will save a gallon of blood. The harder WE push, the more Germans we will kill. The more Germans we kill, the fewer of our men will be killed.\\"<br />-George S. Patton<br /><br />If one did not know that this quote was said by General George S. Patton on June 5, 1944, it could be taken that it was the from a cold-blooded, patriot with no respect for humanity-or at least the Germans. Fortunately, Patton was that, but he also had much more humanity and intellect in him than the Axis powers.<br /><br />Patton begins the chronicle of the General's exploits after the Battle of the Kasserine Pass. As Patton arrives at headquarters, he assigns Omar Bradley as his deputy, and warns him that there are going to be serious changes around the base. He reduces the breakfast time from two hours to fifteen minutes, and instructs all soldiers to wear helmets. He concludes that the reason they have lost the Battle of Kasserine is due to the lack of discipline, and installing it can give the soldiers the gravity of their situation.<br /><br />From the very onset it becomes clear that Patton respects his solider, but only the soldiers that fight fearlessly and selflessly for their country. He has no respect, and in some occasions condemns, the \\"cowards.\\" To Patton anyone complaining of \\"nerves\\" on the battlefield or with self-inflicted wounds is a coward. These individuals do not deserve to be treated to the Army's medical service, as that should be reserved for the most fearless of soldiers who have given their life for their country with no hesitation. Patton is disgusted at these soldiers who complain about the cruelties of war, and treats them with reproach.<br /><br />As odd as it may sound that a General cannot comprehend his soldiers fears and worries, it must be stated that Patton is not in war due to obligation or simply to serve his country. As General Bradley points out, and Patton assents, he \\"loves\\" war, and WWII is the best incident where his ambition can be tested. Unlike many other Generals his outlook towards war is not an event that must be dominated so the peaceful times can roll, but simply an exciting time in history that he must not miss. WWII for Patton is what Woodstock must have been for hippies, a joyous occasion where talent, ambition, and motivation comes together and euphoria occurs. Simply look at the scene where Patton is guiding the tanks through the muddy banks. That scene exemplifies his love of the battlefield, the joy of commanding, and the passion of leading men in battle. World War II may be a curse and abominable incident for many, yet Patton's views this as a chance that occurs every thousand years. He treats war as if it is a football game, and he may not get another chance to prove himself. His view may be attacked because of his somewhat ludicrous outlook on war, but it must understood that not all men join the army out of a sense of honor or obligation-some actually find the experience quite electrifying.<br /><br />Patton's spirituality is one of the oddest parts of his attributes. When he is complimented that he would have made a great general for Napoleon had he lived in the nineteenth century, he argues that he WAS living at that time. Many of his guests are confused at this response, but guffaw anyway. Patton's belief in reincarnation is a big motif of the film, as well as his mention of important historic facts and events-such as the Battle of Zama. Patton is a history aficionado like no one else, and continually refers to previous battles in regard to the regions his troops will overtake. He recounts how these lands were once taken by the Athenians and other respectful ancient powers. Patton seems to envision that all previous rulers conquered and devoured on the battlefield, and now he will continue in their footstep -but this time in the name of the United States of America. As the Germans point out, Patton is a man of the past stuck in the twentieth Century evidenced by his famous quote: \\"God, I hate this century. God, I hate the twentieth Century.\\" The opposition correctly predicts that Patton will invade Sicily because historians have shown that Sicily is the heart of Italy and Patton's ideology is interwoven with history.<br /><br />Patton's outspokenness usually got him in deep trouble, and often limits his position in the army. His dislike for the Russians and dislike of General Bernard Law Montgomery more than often earns him the scorn of the major politicians from back home. It is at times baffling to consider his courage in comparing the Democrats and Republicans to the Nazi's. Patton may have been controversial, at times exaggerating, but he was not necessarily incorrect in his assessments. Though never appearing in the film, Ike's relationship with Patton is an intriguing one. Even after all the controversies that Patton creates for himself, Ike still supports him; though there eventually came a breaking for Ike when the General's unsavory comments left no leeway of support from Ike or any other public figure. It is of note that Patton who once was superior to Omar Bradley and Dwight Eisenhower, eventually became subordinate to both. Though Ike's swift rise to power was due to conditions unrelated to Patton, it can be stated that Patton's outspoken nature may have had some effect-though this wild speculation-in Omar Bradley outranking him. It cannot be overstated that however respected Bradley and Ike where in the eyes of the German's, it was only General Patton that they feared, and for good reason when we consider the losses that he handed the Germans.<br /><br />Not enough praise can be bestowed upon Screenwriter Francis Ford Coppola and Edmund H. North's screenplay that is as wise and ageless as any other screenplay ever committed to film. Even though they won the Academy Award for best Adapted Screenplay, this screenplay is still understated in its clarity and rich dialogue. We can clearly discern that were wise enough to commit a certain portrait of Patton to film. Patton is so delicately written that besides being a grand portrayal of American's most successful and outspoken military man, it comes to life as an inspirational representation of accomplishing one's dreams. Patton loves the battlefield because he deems that fighting is the sole reason to live, and his dislike of cowards must be understood from this angle. He does not treat war as unfortunate due to the fact that he has trained all his life for war, for fighting, and conquering. This film with its grand screenplay, cinematography, and direction still is most renowned and praised due to George C. Scott's portrayal of the general. Anyone who has seen  Dr. Strangelove or: How I Learned to Stop Worrying and Love the Bomb will be taken aback at Scott's controlled yet astonishing portrayal. Often stated as one of the best male performances captured on film, Scott not only gets the Patton's demeanor right, but he completes the harder task of translating the stressful situations that Patton experiences in an illuminating way. Patton does not attempt to show the situations of war, for that has been done countless time, but rather suffices in honoring the men, every one of them, through one figure.ed by his famous quote: \\"God, I hate this century. God, I hate the twentieth Century.\\" The opposition correctly predicts that Patton will invade Sicily because historians have shown that Sicily is the heart of Italy and Patton's ideology is interwoven with history.
